The answer: Undoubtedly, yes.&#13; We face two kinds of stress: acute and chronic. Acute stress is an alarm response, like a â€œjump scareâ€ in a horror movie. Our heart pounds, blood pressure rises, and adrenaline spurts out. Blood is shuttled to our limbs, heart, and brain so we can either fight or flee. Chronic stress is less intense but long-lasting. Thatâ€™s because weâ€™re smart, social creatures who have a lot of downtime to be â€œmiserable to each other and stress each other out,â€ according to Robert Sapolsky, the Stanford neuroendocrinologist and recipient of a MacArthur â€œGeniusâ€ grant.&#13; Our modern world doesnâ€™t have conventional, acute stressors like, as Sapolsky put it, â€œgetting done in by predators.â€ We instead create and propagate chronic stressorsâ€”keeping up with the Joneses, work drama, bills, gossip, that kind of thing. Weight gain, heart disease, stroke, cancer, high blood pressure, high LDL cholesterol, type-2 diabetes, fatigue, depression, osteoarthritis, pain, early death, etc.&#13; Stress eating also, according to Potenza, is why most restrictive fad diets fail. Potenza points to a highly influential study conducted by scientists in the UK. It found that fad dieters who faced heavy work stress crumpled and ate the foods that were off-limits, while their coworkers who werenâ€™t fad dieters didnâ€™t eat any differently when stressed. The all-or-nothing approach seems to make the off-limits foods more attractive and rewarding. The researchers discovered that people who have rigid food rules are less in tune with their bodyâ€™s cues of hunger and fullness, which leads them to overeat far past fullness. Conversely, dieters who were more flexible and didnâ€™t ban foods were less likely to go off the rails and binge, according to other research.*5&#13; &#13; The triggers that lead a person to stress eatâ€”the stressorsâ€”are inevitable. That means strengthening ourselves to deal with discomfort in a different way.&#13; &#13; â€”&#13; Beyond the how much and why a person is eating, Kashey also uses hunger to guide the whatâ€”the actual foods a person eatsâ€”in an unconventional way. First off, he teaches that no foods are off-limits.&#13; â€œKashey helped me remove the morality, guilt, and emotion from my nutrition decision making,â€ said a Navy SEAL who is a client of Kasheyâ€™s. Not all foods are created equal when it comes to mitigating reward hunger and fending off disinhibition eating. â€œA lot of new clients take advantage of the fact that all foods are acceptable, itâ€™s just about moderating the amounts. But thereâ€™s a growing recognition that mitigating it could improve our success. And going for a walk to stave off hunger can only work to a point. Scientists in Australia wondered how foods differed in their ability to make a person feel full. Their hypothesis was that eating foods that are more filling per calorie would satisfy a person before they accidentally overate.&#13; &#13; To test the theory, the researchers chose 38 common foods. There were four different fruits, five different baked goods, seven different snacks, six different high-protein foods, nine different carb-heavy foods, and seven different breakfast cereals.&#13; Participants arrived in the morning, before breakfast. They then ate a 240-calorie serving of one of the 38 foods and reported their hunger levels every 15 minutes for 2 hours. Afterward they went to a breakfast buffet, where the researchers tracked every morsel of food the participants subsequently ate. The experiment was repeated often, with the same participants trying a variety of the foods. (The scientists didnâ€™t include vegetables because vegetables arenâ€™t a staple food, meaning 240 calories of, for example, broccoli would equate to 12 servings of broccoli, an amount that no one actually eats in a meal.)&#13; Foods differed by as much as 700 percent in their ability to fight hunger. Each subject ate the same amount of calories initially. But the subjects eating the more filling foods then ate less at the buffet later onâ€”their calories went further or shorter depending on their food earlier on.&#13; The least filling food was croissants, while the most filling was plain white potatoes. The USDA reports that a small croissant and a medium potato both have about 170 calories. This study suggests youâ€™d have to eat about seven croissants, 1,190 calories, to experience the same fullness youâ€™d get from a single potato. The key quality that made a food filling: how heavy its 240-calorie serving size was.&#13; Kashey thinks of this as the â€œenergy densityâ€ of a food and uses it to help his clients transcend hunger. It leverages our ancestral eating patterns to help us understand real hunger, mitigate reward hunger, improve health, and boost performance. Itâ€™s the same method he used to feed the medal-winning Olympians he coached.&#13; &#13; Itâ€™s easiest to think about this concept as the amount of energy or calories per pound of a given food. â€œSo, for example, at one end of the spectrum thereâ€™s something like iceberg lettuce. There are sixty calories in a pound of iceberg lettuce. At the extreme opposite end of the spectrum there are oils, like olive oil or canola oil. A pound of oil has four thousand calories,â€ Kashey explained. â€œWhen youâ€™re making a direct comparison of these foods, thereâ€™s about a six thousand five hundred percent difference in how many calories a pound of these foods have.â€ Every other food we eat lies between these two foods. Junk food such as chips, candy bars, desserts, and even energy bars, for example, have about 2,000 calories per pound. Processed grains like breads and crackers have about 1,500, while unprocessed grains like cooked rice and oats have 500. Tubers, fruits, and vegetables have about 400, 300, and 120, respectively.&#13; â€œThe reason I belabor this concept with my clients is because of those mechanoreceptors in our stomachs that communicate with our brains to signal fullness,â€ Kashey said. â€œPretend that in a perfect world it takes one pound of food for these mechanoreceptors to be happy. You can see how a person could leverage this to be full on fewer calories.â€&#13; â€œLetâ€™s say a person wants to eat two thousand calories over four meals. Thatâ€™s five hundred calories a meal,â€ said Kashey. For example, if a person were to eat only olive oil for one meal, it would be just a shot glass of oil. â€œThatâ€™s enough energy to fuel your body,â€ he said. â€œBut because that oil takes up so little room in your stomach youâ€™re going to be left hungry. So the body knows something is in our stomach, but does that something have enough energy to power you? Your brain eventually figures out that your stomach is full of stuff thatâ€™s not giving it enough energy to function properly.â€ This is another defense mechanism that kept our species alive. Without it, every time we got hungry we could have eaten, say, mud and been satisfiedâ€”while eventually starving to death.&#13; &#13; â€œSo you lose weightâ€”until your brain responds by sending out mega-cravings for calorie-dense items,â€ said Kashey. â€œAnd that leads to bingeing, which leads to weight gain, paradoxically.â€ Again, the disinhibition effect.&#13; â€œThe question then becomes, What should a person eat? What combinations of foods are optimal? And, importantly, not eating ourselves into supreme comfort each meal.&#13; Kasheyâ€™s logic behind why it works is what makes his approach innovative. Because the foods are so healthful and filling, the body better checks reward hunger and naturally finds the right amount to eat. And a person can fit delicious, dopamine-spiking junk food into his method without guilt or fear of weight gain. They just have to accept that theyâ€™ll likely be hungrier later. This approach respects the idea that foods, after all, are not just vehicles for delivering energy. Foods are often a connection to family, community, culture, and identity, and should never be off-limits.&#13; The method has been validated in the lab. A team of leading researchers at the NIH, for example, found that people placed on a high-energy-density diet naturally ate 500 more calories each day and gained weight while those on the lower-density diet like Kasheyâ€™s lost weight.&#13; The phenomenon has also played out in the real world for thousands of years. Just consider the Kitavans.&#13; In the early 1990s, the Swedish anthropologist Staffan Lindeberg traveled to the island of Kitava, in Papua New Guinea. He was there studying the Kitavan people, a traditional society largely uninfluenced by Western lifestyle. These are people living somewhere between hunter-gatherers and subsistence farmers. Lindeberg wrote, â€œCultivated tubers (mainly yam, sweet potato and taro) are staples, supplemented by fruits, leaves, [coconuts], fish, maize, tapioca and beans.â€ All less calorically dense foods, save for the occasional coconut. About 70 percent of their calories come from carbohydratesâ€”so you could say the Kitavans have a high-carb dietâ€”and they eat around 2,200 calories a day, despite having plenty of food in storage.&#13; &#13; Critically, Lindeberg reported, the Kitavans eat no processed, higher-density foods. He found no overweight Kitavans and zero indications of heart disease or evidence that any Kitavan had ever had a heart attack or stroke. The majority of people he tested were over 50 years old. A handful even reached past 90â€”quite a feat without modern medicine. Meanwhile, in his home country of Sweden, nearly half of the people were overweight or obese, and heart disease and strokes were the top killers. Diet was seemingly the answer.&#13; Lindebergâ€™s was a finding thatâ€™s been repeated often: People who eat a diet that focuses on whole foods experience less disease. The Tsimane people, a tribe in Bolivia, eat rice, plantains, tubers, and corn; meat and fish that they themselves hunt and pull from streams; fruit; and the occasional wild nuts. They register the healthiest hearts ever recorded, according to a global team of scientists. The chronic-disease-free Hadza, in Tanzania, eat mostly wild tubers, fruits, and meats. These general rules also apply to modern industrial societies that eat fewer higher-density foods. The people of Japan are some of the longest living and least likely to die from heart disease and cancer in the developed world, a fact researchers partially credit to their traditional diet of rice, lean proteins, and vegetables.&#13; That a diet based primarily on tubers and whole grains is â€œgood for youâ€ goes against the advice of every low-carb dietâ€”from paleo to keto to Atkinsâ€”pop nutrition stories, and even academic institutions like Harvard. Take how we ruin the nutrition packed into potatoes. Itâ€™s a vessel for gluttony.â€ A pound of French fries, for example, has an energy density of more than 1,500, compared to just 400 for a pound of plain baked potatoes.&#13; In my four months working with Kashey, the Hawthorne effect took full effect.  &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; 12 TO 16 HOURS&#13; HUMANS EVOLVED IN a landscape of feast and famine. Our weight vacillated with the seasons and what nature offered to us. Thankfully weâ€™ve mostly lost the forced famine. But we now seemingly have only two variations of feasting. Weâ€™re either kind of feasting, where we maintain our weight, or weâ€™re definitely feasting, where we add weight. Hunger is missing from our daily, weekly, monthly, and yearly wellness prescription.&#13; Rarely feeling real hunger is a strong sign that a person is suffering from the ill effects of comfort creep, according to a surge of new scientific evidence.&#13; The data shows that we donâ€™t typically gain weight in a linear fashion, like a quarter pound each month for a total of three pounds at the end of the year. Most of us maintain our weight most of the year, then experience periods of gain, according to a study in the New England Journal of Medicine. Scientists have identified big stressors, like getting married, moving, and the holidays, as times where people are most likely to pack on pounds.* For example, the subjects in the study didnâ€™t gain much weight in the fall before Thanksgiving or in the months after the New Year. They did, however, put on anywhere from one to five holiday pounds. And the critical point was that the participants never lost that weight.&#13; &#13; Anthropologists and historians know that our ancestors experienced persistent hunger. But despite what some paleo diet books will have us believe, early humans likely didnâ€™t go for extended stretches without a single calorie. A day at most. And that was rare, according to food historians at Yale.&#13; But it is agreed that these people werenâ€™t eating around the clock. The research suggests they likely ate one or two meals a day. And between meals they surely werenâ€™t snacking on vending-machine foods or sipping Frappuccinos.&#13; Most modern people, on the other hand, start cramming in calories upon waking up and donâ€™t stop until right before bed, Satchin Panda, PhD, a scientist at the Salk Institute for Biological Studies, explained. One of Pandaâ€™s studies found that the average person now eats across a 15-hour window. Research from the University of North Carolina discovered that weâ€™re snacking 75 percent more than we were before 1978. Our snacks are also 60 percent larger and more likely to be ultraprocessed.&#13; The effects of this consistent stream of sugar, salt, and fat and our two versions of feasting are compounded over time, wrote the researchers in the New England Journal of Medicine study. â€œAs this gain is not reversed during spring or summer months,â€ they concluded, â€œ[this] weight gain appears likely to contribute to the increase in body weight that frequently occurs during adulthood.â€ Our growing disconnection from hunger is one of the critical reasons obesity began its rocket-like rise in the late 1970s.&#13; Beyond weight, the trouble with rarely feeling real hunger is that our bodies evolved to leverage lean times for good. Lean times are, in fact, a necessary state for optimizing long-term health. This is because a hungry human body undergoes a sort of cellular natural selection.&#13; We fully metabolize our last meal after 12 to 16 hours, depending on how much we ate. Thatâ€™s when our body releases testosterone, adrenaline, and cortisol: a symphony of hormones that act as signals to burn stored tissues for energy. But we donâ€™t burn our finest tissues. â€œWe get rid of a lot of dead and damaged cells,â€ said Panda.&#13; &#13; Humans have tinkered with hunger as a way to enter a new dimension of religious experience, biological revision, and physical metamorphosis for thousands of years, said Adrienne Rose Bitar, PhD, a historian at Cornell. Medical minds ranging from Hippocrates in 500 BC to American doctors in the 1800s, for example, theorized that stretches without food could help prevent and even fight back against diseases like cancer. In the early 1990s we figured out why there may be a nugget of truth in these old claims.&#13; In 1992, David Sabatini, PhD, MD, a biologist at MIT, discovered whatâ€™s called the mTOR pathway. He told me to think of it as a general contractor, signaling to the body to demolish its old cells and replace them with newer, healthier ones. The bodyâ€™s oldest cells have all sorts of problems and are implicated in many of the diseases that end up killing us.&#13; â€œYou couldnâ€™t fully renovate the old house by bringing in only a plumber, or only an electrician or roofer or drywall guy,â€ he said. â€œYouâ€™d need to hire a general contractor, who would hire all those specialists, who would then come fix all those problems that needed to be fixed.â€&#13; The mTOR pathway senses whether your body is fed or not fed. When you go without food the contractor calls in all his workers. â€œItâ€™s the one way you can trigger a whole series of events that are rejuvenating and antiaging,â€ said Sabatini. Your body is ruthlessly efficient, and it culls the herd by consuming your oldest, weakest cells. A researcher at Cedars-Sinai Medical Center calls this process your bodyâ€™s way of â€œtaking out the trash.â€&#13; These trash cells are ones that no longer divide and are thought to drive aging and disease. A study in Nature said that these cells â€œdisrupt normal tissue function.â€ They cause inflammation, kill healthy cells, induce fibrosis, and inhibit the function of beneficial growth cells. These trash cells â€œactively damage the tissues in which they reside and can be directly linked to features of natural aging,â€ said the scientists. Theyâ€™re also associated with cancer, Alzheimerâ€™s, infections, osteoarthritis, excessive blood sugar and blood lipid levels, and more.&#13; &#13; The bodyâ€™s â€œtaking out the trashâ€ process is officially called autophagy, which translates from ancient Greek as â€œself-devouring.â€ Autophagy is, in many ways, a metaphor for what happens to all things under discomfort: Our weak linksâ€”whether physical or psychologicalâ€”are painfully sacrificed for our good.&#13; Humans probably developed autophagy in concert with day and night cycles, generating what Panda calls circadian rhythms. The research suggests that the body has programmed within it a code to crank up autophagy to repair and rejuvenate itself at night, as it burns through the dayâ€™s food.&#13; But our 15-hour daily eating windows disrupt the process, said Panda. They rob our bodies of the 12 to 16 hours we need to fully metabolize food and lapse into autophagy mode. Or, as the Cedars Sinai scientist put it, â€œIf you eatâ€¦before bed, youâ€™re not going to have any autophagy. That means youâ€™re not going to take out the trash, so the cells begin to accumulate more and more debris.â€&#13; A team of scientists from 16 different institutions including Harvard and Johns Hopkins who studied the topic wrote, â€œFor many of our ancestors, food was probably scarce and primarily consumed during daylight hours, leaving long hours of overnight fasting. With the advent of affordable artificial lighting and industrialization, modern humans began to experience prolonged hours of illumination every day and resultant extended consumption of food.â€&#13; Daily eating marathons also may have caused us to lose a step in our mental game. Somewhat paradoxically, a lack of food typically leads to a surge of energy. â€œThe ability to function at a high level, both physically and mentally, during our extended periods without food may have been of fundamental importance in our evolutionary history,â€ wrote that team of scientists. This is likely why we often define the word â€œhungerâ€ as not just discomfort from a lack of food but also as ambitious drive. Itâ€™s a drive that crosses animalistic distinctions.&#13; &#13; â€œDuring [extended time without food], the body doesnâ€™t shut down, it ramps up,â€ Dr. Jason Fung, a nephrologist and author of The Obesity Code, told me. â€œThink about a hungry wolf versus a lion who just ate. Which one is more focused? The hungry wolf.â€&#13; According to researchers at the University of Southern California, these advantageous responses to hunger first appeared billions of years ago in prokaryotes, microscopic single-celled organisms that were the first life on earth.&#13; Recall the human hunger response of spurting hormones and burning fat. This gives the body energy from fat and from adrenaline, and adrenaline has been shown to increase alertness and focus, said Fung.&#13; Today we donâ€™t have to worry about needing the energy and mental acuity to, say, run down, track, and kill a dik-dik. Hunger may help humans be more focused and productive in the tasks of modern life, according to Panda and Fung. Other research shows that people who stop eating a few hours before bed sleep better, said Panda. â€œSo if you sleep longer and deeper, youâ€™re likely to be more focused the next day.â€&#13; All this research is at odds with fad diet marketing, which has programmed us to ask, â€œWhat should I eat?â€ when we want to improve our health. Going without food and feeling some real hunger is often far more powerful.&#13; Weâ€™re told, for example, that breakfast is the most important meal of the day (often in studies funded by, say, cereal companies). Yet little scientific evidence shows that it has any benefit over any other meal, according to research in the American Journal of Clinical Nutrition. And simply nixing breakfast hits the â€œsweet spot for practicalityâ€ in reacquainting a person with hunger, said Panda. It allows the body to go 12 to 16 hours without a calorie, which goes â€œa long way toward preventing diseases, increasing alertness and energy,â€ said Panda.  &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; 12/31, 11:59:33 P.M.&#13; Iâ€™D BEEN SOBER about 18 months and thought I was done with the big emotional swings. But then some stupid podcast I was listening to on my commute completely defeated me. The host was explaining a concept called the cosmic calendar. It puts all of timeâ€”the universeâ€™s 13.8 billion yearsâ€”on a yearlong scale. So, in the cosmic calendar, the Big Bang occurred on January 1 at 12:00:00 a.m. The Milky Way galaxy formed on March 16. Our solar system took shape on September 2, and Earth followed on September 6, about 4.4 billion years ago. The first complex cells on Earth emerged on November 9. Dinosaurs appeared on Christmas and went extinct on December 30. And then the host said that on this calendar all of recorded human historyâ€”12,000 years and 480 generations of peopleâ€”shows up on the night of December 31 at about 11:59 and 33 seconds.&#13; When I heard that, I felt unfathomably insignificant in the grand scheme of time and space. I could see that I was going to die soon, and that all of the people I care about were also going to die soon. I realized that weâ€™d all be forgotten soon after that. I could see there was nothing I could do about any of it. And I totally lost it.&#13; But at that moment I was missing a higher reality. Like 550,343,279,007.&#13; This figure also doesnâ€™t factor in my luck of being born in a developed country in recent time. Even about a century ago, for example, between 30 and 40 percent of European children died before turning 5. Thatâ€™s why in 1900 the average life expectancy in the world was 31. Now the worldâ€™s average life expectancy is 72.&#13; Yet as modern medicine, comforts, and conveniences have allowed humans more years, weâ€™ve seemingly become less comfortable with death, lifeâ€™s only guarantee. Eight out of ten Westerners say they feel uncomfortable with death. Only half of people over 65 have considered how they want to die.&#13; After someone dies weâ€™re encouraged to â€œstay busyâ€ to â€œtake our mind off it.â€ A dead personâ€™s body is immediately covered and sent to a mortician, where it is either cremated and placed into a shiny new urn or prepared to look as youthful and alive as possible before one final, hourlong viewing, after which it is dropped into the ground of a perfectly manicured cemetery.&#13; Ignoring death wasnâ€™t always the American way, said Gary Laderman, PhD, a death historian at Emory University. â€œIn the nineteenth century and before, Americans were much more intimate with death and it was much more a part of everyday lifeâ€”death was family and community based. Harvard Medical School surgery professor and recipient of a MacArthur â€œGeniusâ€ grant Dr. Atul Gawande notes that 25 percent of all Medicare spending is for the 5 percent of patients in their final year of life. Most of that money goes to treatments that are of little lifesaving benefit and often just put the person through more unnecessary suffering.&#13; We take weird supplements, believe impossible things, and undergo bizarre procedures to try to push death a few days downfield. In my career Iâ€™ve written about men who in the name of living longer have illegally acquired dangerous pharmaceuticals from overseas labs, paid thousands to have the blood of younger men pumped into their bodies, and spent millions funding teams of scientists who will, they believe, discover a fountain of youth in pill form.&#13; Alternatively many of us are so unaware of our impending death that we forget to live true to ourselves, which is one of the most common regrets of Americans on their deathbeds. Our ancestors often rested in the squat position, which required they lightly activate nearly every muscle in their body or else topple over. Or they sat or slept on the ground, which, given its rough nature, forced them to shift around frequently as positions became too uncomfortable. The men in the tribe weigh an average of 112 pounds and the women weigh 95. The American men and women, meanwhile, clocked in at 179 and 164 pounds.&#13; &#13; The satellite data showed that the Hadza men covered about 9 daily miles. Pound for pound, the Hadza burn more than 40 percent more calories a day than Westerners.&#13; The US government recommends that Americans each week get 150 minutes of what they call â€œmoderate to vigorous physical activity,â€ or MVPA. Which was when we were fatter and less muscular than we were the decade before that, and so on. Studies show that prehistoric farmers, for example, were fitter than their ancestors in some ways but not others. But the data shows that early farmers were at least as active as early hunter-gatherers. Most of humanity rather quickly transitioned to farming, and at least 80 percent of civilized people were farmers until the next big shift.&#13; &#13; The second great change in human fitness began around 1850. It marked the start of the Industrial Revolution, and today just 13.7 percent of jobs require the same heavy work as our past days of farming. Roughly three-quarters of jobs are now sedentary, and weâ€™re sitting more every year. Over the last decade, the average American added another hour of daily sitting. Adults now sit for six and a half hours, while kids sit more than eight (the removal of recess hasnâ€™t helped, either). And we donâ€™t sit like our ancestors, squatting and being forced to move around. We melt into plush chairs that require no muscle activation.&#13; When we fully transitioned to effortless work, we did so with those hardwired patterns that favor laziness and make us far less likely to recoup our lost movement. A figure that shows just how predisposed humans are to default to comfort: 2. Thatâ€™s the percent of people who take the stairs when they also have the option to take an escalator.&#13; As the effects of our inactivity began mountingâ€”JFK called us â€œsoft Americansâ€ in 1960â€”we made an attempt to add lost movement back into our days. A typical workout for most people today is zoning out to reality TV while running along a motorized belt in a temperature-controlled room.  &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; â‰¤50 POUNDS&#13; OUR ANCESTORS STARTED walking on two feet, like we do, about 4.4 million years ago. There are about a dozen theories why. But researchers are in agreement that the evolutionary advantages accruing from the ability to carry objectsâ€”food or otherwiseâ€”played a principal role.&#13; Four-legged animals canâ€™t carry well. (Unless, like pack animals, a human straps the weight onto them.) They have to carry or drag items with their mouth, which they canâ€™t do for any appreciable distance.&#13; Primates are unique because we can carry stuff in our hands while we cover ground with our feet. Monkeys, apes, etc., generally suck at this, though. They tend to carry short distances, because for them the act is highly inefficient. It costs a chimp 75 percent more energy to walk the same distance as a human. Which is why those animals mostly cover ground on four limbs and â€œknuckle walk,â€ as anthropologists call it.&#13; When monkeys walk upright itâ€™s with a tipsy, bent-knee, bent-hip gait. Their upper body sways from side to side with each step. Add weight to that wonky walk and it becomes even more inefficient. Humans, on the other hand, can carry up to 15 percent of our body weightâ€”roughly 30 pounds for an average maleâ€”and we still use less energy than other primates, even when theyâ€™re not carrying anything.&#13; Chimps also struggle to grasp and side-carry loads that are just a few pounds. But humans can easily grip heavy weights and walk. Research shows that itâ€™s difficult but totally doable for an average male to side-carry 75 pounds.&#13; &#13; After visiting with Rachel Hopman in Boston, I strolled up to Harvard. I met with Dan Lieberman, one of the worldâ€™s leading anthropologists and a professor at the university. Lieberman studies the evolution of the human body and why weâ€™re built the way we are, especially as it relates to movement and physicality.&#13; His office was Ivy League bordering on clichÃ©. Expansive with lots of oak, way too many academic crests, overstuffed bookshelves, and rich leather couches and chairs that surrounded a coffee table lined with scientific journals. It was at the top floor of Harvardâ€™s Peabody Museum of Archaeology and Ethnology (think: a place where Indiana Jones might store his finds).&#13; Anthropologists since the disciplineâ€™s inception had always believed running played a minimal role in how humans evolved. They considered it something of a useless parlor trick.&#13; The notion made sense. It costs humans twice as much energy to sprint compared to other mammals, and our two legs and upright posture make us pretty slow. The fastest a human has ever covered 100 meters, for example, is in 9.58 seconds. That required an average speed of about 23 miles an hour. And the runner could only sustain that for another handful of meters.&#13; For comparison, a pronghorn antelope can hit 55 miles an hour. A grizzly can do 35. Even the poodle Paris Hilton keeps in her purse can run 30. And those animals can run at those speeds for minutes at a time. Weâ€™re also terrible jumpers, jukers, lifters, and climbers. We two-legged mammals are indeed, as Lieberman puts it, â€œathletically pathetic.â€&#13; But in 2004 he released a study that shook the foundations of both the anthropology and exercise communities. Lieberman found that, no, we canâ€™t go fast. But we can go farâ€”especially in hot weather. The freaks among us can sustain speeds as high as 13 miles an hour for distances over 25 miles. Think: professional marathoners. But even hobbyist runners every weekend finish marathons in three to four hours, averaging about 9 to 6.5 miles an hour. On a hot day a relatively fit human will beat every other mammal in a distance raceâ€”lions, tigers, bears, dogs, etc.*1&#13; &#13; As Lieberman explained in his Nature paper, nicknamed â€œBorn to Run,â€*2 humans can do this thanks to a handful of adaptations we developed over millions of years. We stand on two legs and have springy arches in our feet, long tendons in our legs, big butt muscles, sweat glands across our body, no fur, complicated noses that humidify air before it hits our lungsâ€”the list goes on. These help us run great distances and stay cool while doing it. Other mammals gallop quickly for a few minutes, then need to stop and pant to release heat and cool down.&#13; Endurance was our killer app and as we evolved we used it to our advantage on hot days with persistence hunting: slowly but surely tracking and running down prey for miles upon miles until the animal toppled over from heat exhaustion. Then weâ€™d spear or club it and have dinner.&#13; Liebermanâ€™s paper also suggested that human running mechanics fundamentally changed with the introduction of cushiony, comfortable running shoes in the 1970s. Those shoes typically lead a person to first strike the ground with their heel. Early humans running barefoot likely first struck the ground with the middle or front of their feet. This original foot-striking pattern, according to Lieberman and other anthropologistsâ€™ work, may be more efficient and reduce common running injuries. Lieberman is the man who planted the seed from which the â€œbarefoot runningâ€ movement grew. (Although heâ€™s quick to point out that heâ€™s not a barefoot-running â€œadvocate,â€ just a guy who studies it.)&#13; &#13; I was familiar with Liebermanâ€™s running work. But while training to hoof heavy weight across Alaska, I had contacted him to ask if he knew of any research on early humansâ€™ capacity for load carrying. He was, in fact, investigating that exact topic in his lab and invited me to meet with him. So there I was in his office following up on strength and what role it played for early humans.&#13; â€œStrength is interesting,â€ Lieberman said. â€œBecause thereâ€™s a lot of ideas out there about how important strength is. I think itâ€™s often driven by what people like. There are a lot of people out there who love being in the gym and lifting weights and dislike aerobic activity. They often pump up just how good resistance training is relative to aerobic training. And the reverse is also true. People who do aerobic training and donâ€™t like being in the gym are often dismissive of the importance of strength, right? Itâ€™s kind of a Rorschach test. If you lose muscle quality and canâ€™t move, everything else fails quickly.â€ Thatâ€™s according to Andy Galpin, PhD, who runs the Biochemistry and Molecular Exercise Physiology Laboratory at California State-Fullerton and conducts research for NASA.&#13; Swedish research found that the strongest among a massive group of men of all ages were the least likely to die over two decades. The effect held even when the researchers removed any cardiovascular benefits from strength training. Other research shows that healthy muscle controls blood sugar levels and mitigates inflammation. That condition is something of a pervasive killer, implicated in pretty much all the diseases that end modern people.&#13; Another data set of nearly 2 million healthy people showed that those with the strongest grip and leg strength were 31 and 14 percent less likely to die over two decades.&#13; Nearly all researchers agree that strength and cardio canâ€™t be an either/or proposition. â€œEndurance exercise is not muscle building, and it probably isnâ€™t even muscle maintaining,â€ said Kohrt. Carrying seems to take the average person and toughen them. Stronger heart and muscles. More resilient joints.&#13; &#13; McCarthy recently traveled to the University of Waterloo, where he met with Dr. Stuart McGill. McGill is a leading authority on fitness and back health. â€œItâ€™s no coincidence that the militaries of the world have chosen rucking as the tool to create that physical and mental fusion of toughness,â€ McGill told McCarthy. â€œYou can push someone and really give them a little bit of toughness exposure without high risk of injury.â€&#13; One analysis found that â€œ27% to 70% of recreational and competitive distance runners sustain an overuse running injury during any 1-year period.â€ Our inactivity seems to mess up our movement patterns and cause muscular imbalances. These often lead to injury when people start pounding the pavement.&#13; Scientists at the University of Pittsburgh, for example, investigated what activities most often injure Special Forces soldiers. Running was the top offender. It caused six times more injuries than rucking.&#13; Take the knees. A study in the journal Medicine and Science in Sports and Exercise found running hits the knees with forces 8 times greater than body weight per stride. The same figure for walking is about 2.7. So, in practice, this means that with each running stride a 175-pound person loads their knees with about 1,400 pounds. For walking the figure is about 470.&#13; And despite the initial promises of minimalist and barefoot running, the method for most doesnâ€™t seem to be any less injurious than running in traditional shoes, according to a review of all the research published in the scientific journal Sports Health.*3 â€œThereâ€™s a reason they call it â€˜runnerâ€™s knee,â€™ â€ said McCarthy.&#13; &#13; He consulted with University of Virginia scientists to learn the equivalent knee loads for rucking. If that same person wears a 30-pound ruck, the forces to the knee jump to about 555 pounds each step.*4&#13; That figure isnâ€™t insignificant. But itâ€™s roughly a third of the number for running. And it delivers equivalent cardio benefits, according to researchers at the University of South Carolina.&#13; Walkingâ€™s injury rate is roughly 1 percent. The figure climbs as a person loads a pack. But the risk is comparatively negligible at loads below 50 pounds, according to studies from the British and US militaries. (People who weigh less than 150 pounds may want to use less than 50 pounds, though.)&#13; â€œThe weight in the ruck is also a great equalizer, which also makes it more social,â€ McCarthy said. â€œI ruck with my mom all the time. Thatâ€™s what Homo sapiens evolved to do, and it makes us happy.â€&#13; Humans evolved doing physical work with friends, and sociality is deeply intertwined with effort. Being social while actively hunting and gathering improved our success and survival, according to research in Nature. They load their rucks, herd the kids, leash their dogs, and walk and catch up. There are also group rucks each Wednesday at GORUCK HQ. Sometimes more than 50 people from the Jacksonville area show up.&#13; â€œItâ€™s so easy to integrate rucking into what youâ€™re already doing,â€ said Emily. Ruck in to work. Or to dinner or the coffee shop. Or to the grocery store with a light pack and a food-filled one on the way home. â€œWeâ€™ve drifted into viewing exercise as a 30-minute class we do at a gym or on a machine alone in front of a screen,â€ said McCarthy.&#13; &#13; Raichlen agreed with this notion.  &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; 80 PERCENT&#13; DONNIE REACHES THE teepee first. He dumps his pack, which lands with a slumping thud. And theyâ€™ve mostly all been engineered out of modern life.&#13; â€œSo many people who work out chase infinite cardio or strength capacity,â€ Kelly Starrett, a doctor of physical therapy who consults for various professional sports teams, told me. â€œPeople need just as much movement capacity. Many people go months without taking their joints through a full range of motion. People are de-evolving.â€&#13; Consider a day of the average American office worker. He wakes up on a pillow-topped, waist-high mattress, then slides his legs onto the floor. He shuffles around the house a bit, then moves into a car seat to commute. After sitting at work, the man is back sitting in his car. When he gets home, he sits at a table for dinner, then on the couch for TV. Then itâ€™s back into the horizontal position for bed. Repeat until retirement.&#13; &#13; Katy Bowman, a biomechanist, told me that many bodies today suffer from â€œdiseases of captivity.â€ She compared modern humans to captive killer whales. â€œOrcas in captivity often have fins that flop over,â€ she said. â€œIn the natural world this isnâ€™t an issue. Instead of flopped fins, our outcome is poor movement, pain, and chronic diseases.&#13; And a personâ€™s movement is only as bad as theyâ€™ve made it, said Bowman. Kids have full command of their joints and can easily squat, lunge, lift overhead, and more. But movement is a use-it-or-lose-it proposition. Those kids eventually sit at school desks, then join average Americans behind a work desk. But, as Mayo Clinic researchers put it, â€œThe human, simply put, was not designed to sit all day.â€&#13; But through movement we bloom. Research suggests that moving through full ranges of natural motion may jump-start dormant cells that fight aging. Conversely, a lack of complete movement can potentially cause cellular maladaptations, making people more likely to age poorly, according to a study in Medicine &amp; Science in Sports &amp; Exercise.&#13; Rediscovering lost movement, a swell of research is showing, can fix one of the most insidious diseases of captivity: back pain (to take just one example).&#13; About 80 percent of Americans will experience back pain sometime in their life. A quarter of people say theyâ€™ve had it in the last few months. Itâ€™s the most common place people experience pain, and the most frequent reason people see a doctor or take a sick day from work. Back pain costs our economy $100 billion every year.&#13; Back pain sometimes comes from something a doctor can scan, see on an image, and diagnose. Like an injured disk, tumor, osteoporosis, or fracture. But 85 percent of it is labeled â€œnonspecific,â€ which is unseeable pain that appears from the ether. Scientists at Harvard estimate that 97 percent of this nonspecific back pain is caused by the way we now live. By captivity in our modern environment.&#13; &#13; When I met with the anthropologist Daniel Lieberman he explained to me that much of this peculiar back pain exists on a U-shaped curve.&#13; Picture a graph that shows pain on the y axis and activity level on the x axis. The data will be shaped like a U. This means that the groups with the most pain are both the least and most active. The people with the least amount of pain are at the bottom of the U and they have a medium activity level. Case in point, Lieberman said, is that studies show that people who perform what we think of as â€œbackbreaking workâ€ experience roughly the same amount of back pain compared to office workers. For example, 38 percent of farmers in northern China experienced back pain over a handful of months, while the number was anywhere from 33 to 46 percent among Chinese office workers. Research from Australia, meanwhile, discovered that people who do a broad range of physical activity are less likely to experience pain.&#13; This may seem to suggest that too much activity is bad. But other data reveals the truth. Back pain from â€œtoo muchâ€ seems to be due to performing one physical activity at the expense of all others. â€œProbably from very weird, bizarre kinds of movements that people didnâ€™t do in the past,â€ said Lieberman. â€œNo one had to lift Amazon Prime boxes all day.â€ Too little activity is quite a similar problem, except itâ€™s caused by a weird, bizarre lack of movement. A life of sitting, standing, and lying down.&#13; â€œWe used to be very active movement generalists,â€ said Bowman. But weâ€™ve now outsourced most of our movement to machines, chairs, soft beds, and more. When our work does require movement, itâ€™s often specific, repetitive, and destructive. â€œThere are almost no remaining â€˜movement generalistsâ€™ who are meeting their daily movement needs anymore,â€ said Bowman.&#13; &#13; Even our past inactive moments werenâ€™t entirely lazy. Research shows that hunter-gatherer tribes actually rest just as much as we do. Each of our muscles slackens like weâ€™ve gone brain dead. â€œBut our bodies arenâ€™t well adapted for chairs,â€ said Lieberman.&#13; David Raichlen ran a study where he tested how hunter-gatherersâ€™ resting positions influenced their muscle activity. They mostly squat or sit on the ground. â€œSquatting or sitting without their backs resting, unsurprisingly, increased their lower-back-muscle activation significantly,â€ said Raichlen. Resting in a squat or kneeling lightly engages all the muscles in the lower body and torso. Sitting on a stool requires less work, but a person must still engage their core and back muscles to stay upright.&#13; The takeaway: â€œHuman physiology likely evolved in a context that included substantial inactivity, but increased muscle activity during sedentary time, suggesting an inactivity mismatch with the more common chair-sitting postures found in contemporary urban populations,â€ Raichlen wrote in the study. This theory is called the inactivity mismatch hypothesis.&#13; Our comfortable, supportive-to-the-extreme chairs, couches, and beds of today do the work that our muscles are meant to. And muscle is use-it-or-lose-it stuff. Just ten days of not using a muscle significantly weakens and shrinks it.&#13; Then when chair-weakened people bend over to lift something or move into a new position, their body is brittle. It breaks. And this is likely a critical reason why back pain is so common in the most comfortable societies and essentially absent among movement generalists. Populations in Asia and the Middle East who rest and do many activities in the squatting position, for example, see little to no hip and lower-back issues.&#13; &#13; When our modern pain arises, we donâ€™t listen to what itâ€™s trying to tell us. Pain was and still is an evolutionary advantage. Itâ€™s our brainâ€™s way of telling us weâ€™re doing something potentially dangerous. A warning of harm and threat. A use of discomfort to suggest a change that will improve our health and safety. Yet we mute it with pills, surgery, or rest. Those are easy treatments, but evidence shows theyâ€™re usually not a solution. â€œRest, opioids, spinal injections, and surgeryâ€¦will not reduce back-related disability or its long-term consequences,â€ wrote a global team of 12 doctors and scientists who studied all the evidence on back pain treatment.&#13; Back pain is one of the leading reasons for opioid prescriptions. Yet the scientists found that pills only temporarily mute pain and donâ€™t work over the long term. For more relief, people must keep popping more, stronger pills. Treating back pain with powerful painkillers was, in fact, a key driver of the opioid epidemic, according to the research.&#13; Then thereâ€™s surgery. Forget the cost. Researchers at the University of Cincinnati Medical School tracked roughly 1,500 workers who had debilitating back pain that was keeping them from work. Other times itâ€™s raw. Theyâ€™re outside always. The Hadza also bathe and wash their hands, rarely, in muddy, sometimes manure-filled puddles. They shit outside. Probably have gotten piss on themselves, too.&#13; This lifestyle might seem like a quick path to a deathly infection. But the Hadza are seemingly impervious to some of the diseases that take down many of us Westerners. They donâ€™t seem to get Crohnâ€™s, colitis, IBD, or even colon cancer. The first three diseases have been increasing rapidly in the developed world, and are now spreading into developing countries as they westernize.&#13; Doctors are particularly concerned about the rise of colon cancer. Itâ€™s already the third-most-common cancer. But itâ€™s increasingly becoming a cancer of young, otherwise-healthy people. A person born in 1990, for example, has double the risk of colon cancer and quadruple the risk of rectal cancer compared to someone born in 1950. Scientists at the University of Texas, Austinâ€™s MD Anderson Cancer Center project that colon cancer will rise by 90 percent among 20- to 34-year-olds over the next decade. A young personâ€™s risk is still low. But younger people are more likely to die from the disease, because itâ€™s often too far advanced by the time docs catch it.&#13; There may be an answer. Studies around an emerging theory called the hygiene hypothesis have strongly linked the rise in these diseases and others to our supersanitized lives. Even mood, metabolism, and immunity are affected.&#13; The West began its all-out war on germs in the 1800s. This is when we realized that some germs are the source of infectious disease. This battle has saved many lives and raged ever since. But itâ€™s also had some unintended consequences.&#13; â€œWe have this notion that germs cause disease. And scientists estimate that fewer than 100 of these species could hurt your health.&#13; As we evolved we developed a mutually beneficial alliance with many of these microscopic living organisms. Itâ€™s exactly how vaccines work. But research shows that most Americans consume a limited variety of foods. Our most commonly eaten foods, for example, are made mostly of refined flour, which has the fiber stripped from it. But a study of the Hadza, for example, found that they eat more than 600 foods, 70 percent of them unprocessed, fiber-filled plants.&#13; &#13; â€œI eat a lot of organic plants,â€ said Schnorr. â€œI eat many of them raw.â€ Cooking not only kills microbes, it can also slightly lower fiber counts. Researchers have long studied groups around the world for their â€œharder to killâ€ traits. The Ama, or sea women, of Japan and Korea first came on the radar of the US Department of Defense in the 1960s as it was establishing the US Navy SEALs. The women are a particularly interesting example of what happens to humans who repeatedly expose themselves to uncomfortable environments.&#13; My showerâ€”30 minutes and scorchingâ€”provided something else I hadnâ€™t experienced in a month: warmth. Constant climatic comfort is something else we also may want to rethink.&#13; Roughly two or three thousand years ago, women in the tiny fishing villages of Japan and Korea began diving into the cold waters of the Japan Sea and Pacific Ocean. No wetsuits. No breathing contraptions. The women would strip down to a loincloth, diving trunks, or nothing at all. Theyâ€™d row or swim to a spot where the oceanâ€™s rocky bottom was 10 to 90 feet below, and dive, scouring the cold, clear ocean depths.&#13; After a minute or three, the womanâ€™s hand would emerge from the surf and clasp the side of her boat. Then her other hand would rise, dumping into the boat a bucket of edible sea treasures like abalone, uni, mussels, or seaweed. In the summer the Ama worked six- to ten-hour days on the chilly water, doing more than 150 daily dives. From late fall to early spring, the ocean water dropped to just 50 degrees and the air temperatures could be just a couple degrees above freezing. But the Ama dove anyway, constantly pushing their limits to discover the knife edge of exposure.&#13; Department of Defense research on the Ama showed they had less incidence of 14 of the 16 illnesses the scientists studied. Compared to their fellow villagers, the women were less likely to catch a cold, to get heart disease, to get arthritis, to get liver or kidney diseases, and so on. And the diseases they had were hazards of the jobâ€”like hearing loss due to the pressure of the ocean on their eardrums. Other researchers discovered that the Ama also had larger lung volumes, stronger muscles, and better endurance. Not surprising for breath-holding swimmers.&#13; &#13; Findings on the Ama forced researchers to reconsider the rules of physiology. Humans lapse into hypothermia when their core temperature drops to 95 degrees or below. But the Amaâ€™s winter core temperatures averaged a physiology-bending 94.5 degrees.&#13; The scientists also wondered how all that time in the cold impacted the Amaâ€™s metabolisms. A cold body, after all, ignites a complex network of calorie-burning internal furnaces to ensure that its organs donâ€™t become dangerously chilled. So they picked at random 20 Ama and 20 villagers and invited them into a makeshift lab to have their metabolic rates tested. The data showed that the Ama burned an additional 1,000 calories a day.&#13; Thanks in large part to the Ama research, scientists now know whatâ€™s driving their 1,000-calorie burn: brown fat.&#13; Brown fat is a metabolically active tissue. Brown fat in the cold acts like a furnace that burns our white fat (the type we try to lose with diet and exercise) to generate heat. Working brown fat cranks through more calories than working our muscles and brain. Which is exactly why a team of scientists in the Netherlands think that getting comfortable with the cold can be an effective weight-control tactic.&#13; The bad news, the scientists say, is that our creature comforts have rendered brown fat moot.&#13; â€œIn the past century several dramatic changes in the daily living circumstances in Western civilization have occurred, affecting health. For example, we are much better able to control our ambient temperature,â€ wrote the scientists. â€œ[We] lack exposure to varied ambient temperature [because we] cool and heat our dwellings for maximal comfort while minimizing our body energy expenditure necessary to control body temperature.â€&#13; &#13; The scientists call this burning of energy â€œnon-shivering thermogenesis.â€ Research shows it can elevate metabolism anywhere from a small percentage to 30 percent. Which is why the scientists write, â€œSimilar to exercise training, we advocate temperature trainingâ€¦.More-frequent cold exposure alone will not save the world, but [it] is a serious factor to consider.â€&#13; The cost of leveraging the power of brown fat is, of course, braving the cold. The upside is that we donâ€™t have to dive into a frigid ocean for hours at a time to see a substantial benefit.&#13; The research shows that anyone can become cold acclimatized. I noticed this with William and Donnie. People who spend a lot of time in colder temperatures, scientists say, are less impacted by temperature extremes. We need a week or two of exposure to reach the point where we feel comfortable in the cold and begin optimizing our cold furnaces.&#13; In winter, the scientists recommend people lower their thermostats by three to four degrees each week. This slowly pushes our comfort zone, allowing us to adapt without unnecessary suffering. Then we can stop once weâ€™re living in 64 degrees. Another study conducted by the NIH found that people who slept in rooms in the mid-60s saw a 10 percent increase in their metabolic activity. They also saw improvements in health markers like blood sugar levels. A person can go full-on Ama if they want, by taking ice baths. Some do (and, of course, dramatically Instagram it). But it seems like overkill, in light of the research.&#13; Cedars-Sinai, Johns Hopkins, and other leading medical research institutions are even finding that extreme cold can help prevent severe brain damage and death after dangerous medical events. Doctors bring a cardiac arrest patientâ€™s body temperature down to between 89 and 93 degrees for roughly 24 hours. This sets off a cascade of events that protect a compromised brain, like lowering its demand for oxygen and energy, preventing neuronal cell death, and decreasing inflammation and harmful free radicals.&#13; &#13; â€”&#13; &#13; The Sherpas of Nepal are another group that is forcing scientists to rethink the limits of the human body and how it responds to extreme environments.&#13; Dr. Andrew Murray has spent thousands of hours trekking the worldâ€™s highest mountains. The views are nice. But as a physiologist, he said heâ€™s always been more fascinated by the people carrying his stuff. â€œYouâ€™ll be huffing and puffing your way up what looks like a fairly gentle slope, but youâ€™re held back by the low oxygen,â€ he told me. â€œThen a porter will breeze by you. Heâ€™s maybe much older than you, and heâ€™s carrying your bags and other peopleâ€™s bags, walking like itâ€™s a stroll in the park.â€&#13; The Sherpa, an ethnic group in eastern Nepal, are most famous for this high-altitude fitness. Although the sport of mountaineering was pioneered by Westerners, Sherpas hold the world record for the most Everest ascents as well as the most summits without supplemental oxygen. They also hold the majority of speed summiting records. Pemba Dorje Sherpa climbed from Everestâ€™s South Base Camp to its summit with supplemental oxygen in 8 hours and 10 minutes, while Kazi Sherpa completed the feat without supplemental oxygen in 20 hours and 24 minutes.&#13; Murray recently conducted a study to see if Sherpa fitness was solely from years of mountaineering, or if perhaps the extreme land that the Sherpas come from had given them some sort of edge. The existing data showed a paradox.&#13; As the average person gains altitude, their body responds by producing more oxygen-carrying red blood cells. Yet, curiously, previous studies found that Sherpas do ramp up their red blood cell production when climbing, but not at nearly the rate of lowlanders. Which means Sherpas actually register less oxygen in their blood than we do while climbing.&#13; &#13; To solve the mystery, Murray took thigh muscle biopsies on a group of Sherpas and Westerners at low altitudes. The groupsâ€”who were matched for age, sex, and general fitness levelâ€”then trekked from Katmandu to Everest Base Camp. Once they arrived at the 17,600-foot camp, the scientists took the same biological measurements.&#13; The biopsies showed that at altitude the Sherpasâ€™ mitochondriaâ€”tiny power plants within human cells that power our bodiesâ€”produced more ATP, or energy, using less oxygen. They also found that the Sherpas used fat as fuel more efficiently.&#13; â€œItâ€™s interesting because the Sherpas are actually unremarkable at sea level,â€ said Murray. â€œYou donâ€™t see them winning marathons. Their adaptation is not one that gives them super performance at sea level, but it does at altitude when the oxygen is scarce.â€&#13; In other words, Westerners have the engine of a gas-guzzling SUV, while the Sherpas are more like a sensible hybrid that sips fuel. When fuel is abundantâ€”at low altitudeâ€”both engines get the job done. But when you climb into a fuel-scarce, high-altitude environment, the more efficient engine is optimal. It helps the Sherpas climb farther, faster, and with less effort.&#13; Even more striking, the team of scientists retook the measurements of both groups after theyâ€™d spent two months at Everest Base Camp. The findings showed that the energy levels in the muscles of the lowlanders dropped. Yet, like a flower exposed to the sun, the Sherpasâ€™ muscle energy levels bloomed, steadily increasing despite having less oxygen.&#13; Murray published his results in Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences, and said that Nepali Sherpas have evolved to perform like superhumans at altitude.&#13; Murrayâ€™s findings are an important first step in developing treatments for intensive-care-unit patients suffering from hypoxia, or a lack of oxygen. Hypoxia is life-threatening and occurs in healthy people at high altitude, and also in critical-care patients. Currently, doctors add oxygen to these patientsâ€™ blood. But that thickens the blood and can cause complications like clogged blood vessels.&#13; &#13; Murrayâ€™s research is in its infancy, but the ultimate goal is to develop a method that allows patients to become more Sherpa-like and use what little oxygen they have more efficiently. This would lead to better health outcomes in sick peopleâ€”and, perhaps, better performance in athletes.&#13; We can experience similar benefits by living and training at altitude, according to an article in the scientific journal Sports Medicine. Endurance coaches since the 1960s have looked for a competitive edge by having their athletes â€œtrain high, race low,â€ which ramps up oxygen-carrying red blood cell counts.&#13; But the Sports Medicine research team found that altitude training does far more than that. It also leads to changes in mitochondria, which make our muscles more efficient, and improves how we buffer exercise-induced acids, allowing us to go harder longer. The catch is, a person canâ€™t just spend a weekend in the mountains and expect to emerge like a Sherpa. Prolonged, repeated bouts at altitudeâ€”mountain misogis, perhapsâ€”lead to the most profound changes.&#13; &#13; â€”&#13; One of the most promising ongoing research projects into the future of discomfort science is happening in Iceland. Shortly after I returned from Alaska, I traveled there to meet a man studying one of Earthâ€™s most hard-to-kill populations. He, in fact, is part of them.&#13; Iâ€™d heard rumors of Dr. Kari Stefansson: In his 70s and built like an NFL tight end at six foot six and 220 pounds of raw muscle. One hundred percent Icelandic, with the white hair and blue eyes and everything youâ€™d expect. Also brilliant.&#13; Stefansson ran the Harvard neurology department for a while, until he left to open deCODE, a 190-scientist-strong genetic research company housed in a modern mega-laboratory in Reykjavik. The place holds more data than all of the countryâ€™s banking system. Stefansson and his team have sequenced the genotypes of 60 percent of living Icelanders, and their scientific studies have been cited about 200,000 times. This work has made Stefansson one of the richest men in Iceland and a national celebrity.&#13; &#13; Stefansson is a great explorer of the human genome who is searching for information that could lead to treatments for many of the diseases that kill us. Iceland happens to be the perfect spot for his work.&#13; Iceland is a lot like the Hotel California, in the sense that once people check in, they never leave. A handful of people arrived in Iceland about 1,100 years ago and populated the island, and few have come and gone since. The majority of Icelanders come from a single family tree. Itâ€™s so common for Icelanders to have unknown cousins that the government created a genealogical dating app so people can avoid familial hookups.&#13; For a geneticist like Stefansson, Iceland and its people are a scientific wellspring. With few people migrating in or out, there is less genetic variation among Icelanders. Therefore there is less confusing background noise in the data. The place offers a massive, naturally occurring control group.&#13; This means that Stefansson and his team can more easily track diseases as they transfer through family lines. From there they can single out the genes that could lead people to get sick and die. The research done by deCODE has discovered genesâ€”single bad actors among 3 billionâ€”involved in heart disease, Alzheimerâ€™s, schizophrenia, and cancer.&#13; But thereâ€™s a flip side; deCODE has also found some genes that could cause humans to be well and live longer. And thatâ€™s what Stefansson is really interested in. He found in the APP gene a variant that offers complete protection against age-related mental decline and Alzheimerâ€™s disease. And a variant in the ASGR1 gene that gives its holders significant protection from heart disease. Heâ€™s found other variants that greatly reduce the risk of developing diabetes and prostate cancer.&#13; &#13; Stefansson has also come to believe that there might be another gene lurking within Icelanders that makes them tougher than the rest of us.&#13; The World Health Organization recently discovered that Icelandic men are the longest living on Earth. Guys from Iceland rack up roughly 81.2 years. Thatâ€™s 13.2 more years than the global average and 5.2 more years than men in the United States. When a team of 500 researchers from more than 300 institutions in 50 countries combined all their longevity data, Icelandic men outlasted all others.&#13; The answer isnâ€™t likely cultural. Icelandâ€™s healthcare system is nothing special; most rankings donâ€™t list it as a standout. Its people arenâ€™t exactly runway models; they fall in the middle of the pack for obesity rates. They are in fact heavy eaters, consuming an average of 3,260 calories a day and eating fewer fruits and vegetables compared to other European countries. And thereâ€™s no strong evidence indicating that Icelanders are more active than most other nations. Stefansson believes Icelandic longevity may have something to do with the story of its people.&#13; In the year 874, the Vikings of northern Norway were sick of a quarrelsome king from the south who had taken over the country. So about 4,000 to 6,000 of the most pissed-off, risk-prone men in the north loaded themselves, sheep, cattle, and horses into Viking longboats and launched. They first sailed to the Shetland Islands and Ireland. There they kidnapped women (they were Vikings, after all), which grew their numbers to about 8,000. The Vikings then set back out into the North Atlantic, searching for a home.&#13; This was no Carnival cruise. Viking longboats were narrow and light, with shallow hulls. Fast, but they could easily capsize in bad weather. And the advanced storm-warning techniques of the time mostly included praying and hoping.&#13; But these Vikings were then the worldâ€™s most advanced navigators. After a miserable five days at sea they found a destination. It was a patch of volcanic rock about the size of Kentucky that was covered in jagged rock, ice, and moss. It was constantly hit with wind, rain, snow, and cold. It was engulfed in darkness three-quarters of the year. It was devoid of edible life. It was now home. They called it Iceland.&#13; &#13; A group of Celtic monks had once tried to live on the severe island. But theyâ€™d mysteriously vanished, never to be seen or heard from again.&#13; Over the next 100 years, another 20,000 disaffected Norwegian men and kidnapped UK women arrived. All of these settlers quickly faced a jarring reality: â€œIceland is a shit place to live,â€ as one Icelander described it to me.&#13; The most the settlers could grow was hay and grass, which theyâ€™d feed to sheep and cattle. So they ate sheep, cattle, and dairy products, and not a whole lot else. And there was never enough of that food.&#13; The winters were nine months long. The country saw rain, hail, or snow 213 days a year. Winds regularly hit 40 miles an hour, sometimes reaching into the 100s.&#13; The longest days in the dead of winter offered just four to five hours of dusky sunlight. As another Icelander explained to me, â€œIn winter the sun comes up to say, â€˜Fuck you,â€™ and then drops back down.â€&#13; And that was just the everyday, uncomfortable white noise of living in Iceland. Sometimes the country pushed people over the edge.&#13; â€œWe know that living in this country for eleven hundred years has changed us, and we have evidence for that,â€ said Stefansson while we drove the streets of Reykjavik in his black Porsche SUV. â€œIn many ways, itâ€™s inevitable. Because humans are ultimately a consequence of a bunch of DNA macromolecules and the environment we live in.â€&#13; Itâ€™s the combination of DNA and environmentâ€”this formula of life and how we live in itâ€”that determines our fate. Consider Julian and Adrian Riester, identical twins born in 1919. They had the same genetic codeâ€”and the same lifestyle, both becoming Catholic monks. They went to the same schools, ate the same meals, did the same tasks, etc. Their formula of life and living it were identical. They died of the same disease within hours of each other on June 8, 2011.&#13; &#13; Discomfort is likely a key ingredient in the Icelandic formula, Stefansson was telling me. â€œIt was never an easy time living here these eleven hundred years. There were volcanic eruptions. We lived in unheated houses for a thousand years in a very, very cold country. We had to earn a living by fishing in a rough sea. We faced infectious epidemics,â€ Stefansson said, piloting the Porsche past HallgrÃ­mskirkja, a towering stone cathedral that reaches into the turbulent Icelandic sky. â€œAnd so what has that done to us?â€ he asked.&#13; Itâ€™s a question thatâ€™s long intrigued Stefansson as well as many others on the island. â€œOur history is characterized by many setbacks,â€ Dr. Ottar GuÃ°mundsson, an Icelandic historian, told me.&#13; â€œBecause of this harshness, there was no population increase here for many centuries,â€ he said. Icelanders are now living anywhere from two to four years longer than the men of the countries from which the first Icelanders came.&#13; And thatâ€™s likely â€œa consequence of this merciless little island,â€ said Stefansson. â€œThis fucking wet rock in the North Atlantic that has been punishing us relentlessly for the last eleven hundred years.â€ He pulled up to my hotel. â€œAnd this is not a superficial, esoteric statement.â€&#13; &#13; Icelandâ€™s crucible of discomfort and disasters may have culled the herd. Natural selection suggests the people who couldnâ€™t hack it likely perished. Those with a high discomfort tolerance probably thrived. By happenstance, genetic drift (the term for random chance and catastrophic events leading to specific traits becoming more frequent in a population) would have altered the small, isolated countryâ€™s gene pool in such a way that some ideal genes may have been given an opportunity to spread faster.&#13; The result is that Icelanders may have buried within their genetic code a harder-to-kill gene, one that explains their longevity. If Stefansson can isolate this theoretical gene or set of genes, perhaps he and his team can figure out a way to bring it to the masses.&#13; &#13; â€”&#13; When I arrived home in Las Vegas, I had to immediately shift back to modern life and start chopping at all the work that had piled up over the weeks. Nothing seems to bother you now.â€&#13; &#13; Later that night I considered what she said. I surely felt different since my return. But I also knew I was affected by my past year digging into what weâ€™ve lost with modern comforts.&#13; Most obviously, I felt more aware. At a skin-deep level, this showed itself as a newfound appreciation for the incredible comforts of our modern world. My first week back Iâ€™d break out into an idiotic grin every time I turned on a faucet or drove a car or ate food that wasnâ€™t reconstituted sludge cooked and served in a plastic bag.&#13; But on a deeper level I felt an awareness of time, how little of it we have, and what that can tell us about how we should use it. Marcus Elliott told me that a critical benefit of misogi is what he called â€œcreating impressions in your scrapbook.â€ â€œIf youâ€™re seeing and doing all the same things over and over, your scrapbook looks pretty empty when you take inventory of your life,â€ he said. â€œSo we need to do more novel things to start creating more impressions in our scrapbooks, so we donâ€™t feel like the years are flying by. I mean, you remember every single detail of novel, meaningful experiences. You have no chance to forget them the rest of your life.â€&#13; About a 19-mile, open-water Pacific Ocean swim misogi Nelson Parrish said, â€œAs an artist, I thought I knew blue. But that misogi fully immersed me in so many shades, gradients, vibrancies, and transitions of blue. The water and sky. I now know blue. The experience drastically impacted my art, and Iâ€™ll never forget those blues.â€&#13; The difficulty and new challenges in Alaska left me with a massive new file of memories to relive and stories to tell. Iâ€™d experienced firsthand the phenomenon first theorized by William James and proven by recent studies, which shows that new events decelerate our perception of time.&#13; I found myself applying these two lessons to my everyday life. I was thinking less and noticing more. I sought more connection, silence, and solitude both at home and in nature. I spent less time in front of screeens and was more of an active listener in conversations with my wife and family. At least twice a week Iâ€™d do a ruck in the desert and find a sort of sustained Zen traveling across the miles of red-rock-and-cactus-flanked trails. And my wife was right: I could see that my modern â€œproblemsâ€ werenâ€™t real problems, so I was harder to rattle. Chasing that which makes humans harder to kill was, it seemed, making it easier for me to live.&#13; &#13; In sobriety thereâ€™s something called the â€œpink cloudâ€ phenomenon. It describes the intense feelings of awareness, euphoria, connectedness, confidence, and calmness that occur in the early stages of recovery, right after a person has gone through the most uncomfortable phases of drying out. We realize weâ€™ve pulled ourselves out of a slow death and become eager to live.&#13; But the pink cloud eventually fades and real life sets in, leading many people to see if higher relief was in the bottle after all. My own pink cloud lasted about two years, until normalcy set in. I didnâ€™t fall off the wagon, but I did become somewhat restless and discontented.&#13; Back from misogi, I felt like I was back on the pink cloud. Alaska provided me with another heavy dose of discomfort, and its lessons changed me. But I also understood that they wouldnâ€™t be everlasting, that comfort creep would gain inches each day. Iâ€™m already planning the next misogi.&#13; Iâ€™m also now finishing this book in the midst of a global pandemic during which few would argue that they feel overly â€œcomfortable.â€ Too many have died, many more have been seriously ill, and millions beyond that have lost their livelihoods. But just as the pandemic forced nature itself to experience a sort of rewilding, from the cleaner canals of Venice to coyotes roaming around the mostly empty Golden Gate Bridge, we all did, too. It was a reminder that weâ€™re all still deeply connected to the natural world and that our technological advancements canâ€™t fix everything immediately. But it was also a rare break from the predictable. A moment for reflection, re-prioritizing, and maybe change.&#13; &#13;
